               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-261432">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261432">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/84f1664548b34e1466b7ca76747276a5?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Chris Winter
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    The Ward-Brownlee
                    <i>
                     Rare Earth
                    </i>
                    hypothesis is an interesting one. I think its basically right, but at the same time I think the authors are way too pessimistic. If complex life evolves on only one in a million habitable worlds, or one in ten million, that still makes it likely that multiple civilizations are out there.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    How long a technical civilization is likely to last is a vexing question. If, as seems possible (probable to some) they are likely to use that technology to destroy themselves, the chances of survival will be enhanced if they have self-sufficient offworld outposts.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Personally, I acknowledge that the picture for civilization on our small planet looks somewhat grim at the moment, especially if you rely only on strict extrapolation of trends. But I think we will at some point find the will and the means to bend those trends in a more favorable direction.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261432">
                      October 30, 2010 at 3:58 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">

               <!-- #comment-## -->
               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-261433">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261433">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/84f1664548b34e1466b7ca76747276a5?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Chris Winter
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Methodissed wrote (#12):
                    <i>
                     Belief in the inevitability of human kind visiting distant solar systems is a matter of irrational faith.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Thats right.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     Given our behavior on the planet today, this belief is completely unfounded.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Thats not right.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Faith in performing some physical feat is completely unfounded only if doing it would violate the laws of physics. Interstellar travel will be enormously expensive and difficult, but it is physically possible. I would not even call the hope for such things as warp drive completely unfounded. We have discovered new laws of physics in the past; we now can cross the ocean in a day and speak to almost anywhere on Earth in an instant. Five generations ago, both those abilities were fantasies. In my view, its irrational to think weve discovered all the physical laws there are.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     Were destroying our ecosystem with great efficiency, were gobbling up finite resources as though they are endless, were reproducing like rabbits and cannot feed our population, we engage in perpetual war and are at risk of annihilating our entire species, etc. etc.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    All this is true; but not everyone behaves like this. Nor is any of these problems insoluble.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     This James T. Kirk fantasy about exploring the galaxy is misguided and irresponsible. At our current rate of destruction, society as we know it wont last a couple centuries  well be lucky to survive this one.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Its interesting that one core assumption of the Star Trek canon is the narrow escape of humanity from knocking itself back to permanent savagery in the 22nd century. As for exploring the galaxy, thats a male dream just as was finding the New World in 1490 or heavier-than-air craft in 1900. Ive read and reviewed books by two women who regard the Apollo moon missions as an adolescent distraction from real life. Missions of exploration may at times be ill-advised, but they are not inherently unwise or wasteful.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     Lets keep ourselves grounded and focus on problems that we can realistically attain. Pie-in-the-sky fantasies belong in sci-fi books and movies, not science blogs.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    The problem with this is that theres no universal agreement on whats realistically attainable. Take climate change. Many today believe that doing anything about the excess CO2 were pumping into the atmosphere would be ruinously expensive. (Or at least they profess to believe that.) Experience with other environmental problems gives the lie to this gloomy view.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    We will always have problems on Earth. We could spend every cent of every nations GDP on alleviating them and still not fix them all. Indeed, its often been the seemingly frivolous (to some) investments in exploration or pure science that have given us feasible solutions to previously intractable problems.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261433">
                      October 30, 2010 at 4:33 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>

The B-type star was originally less massive and luminous until it accreted mass and angular momentum from its companion star. If you work out the likely evolution of the progenitor system, Regulus was probably originally a binary of two A-type stars and is likely around a billion years old.

                   <p>
                    I didnt know they thought it was originally an A type binary system. If I may ask please, whats your source for that?
                   </p>
                   <p>

                    <i>
                     (which Ill link separately for yall)
                    </i>
                    However my guess there was that the binary* had originally been an even more massive blue dwarf as well as still late B-type Regulus. Regulus now has somewhere between 3 to 5 solar masses
                    <i>
                     (different sources give different figures)
                    </i>
                    and a spectral type of B7 V.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    I guess the Algol paradox   where stellar interaction and matter / mass exchange affects the stars evolution and a stars age doesnt seem to be what it should be  strikes again.
                    <img alt=";-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_wink.gif"/>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    As one of the more familar, bright and at 77 or 78 light years the very nearest B type star I was using Regulus as an example of that type but youre right I guess I couldve found a better example. Acrux, Mimosa (Beta Crucis), Alycone (the brightest of the Plieades) or Spica maybe.
                   </p>
                   <p>

                    * Actually triple as theres a distant red dwarf star sharing the Regulan system too.

               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-261438">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261438">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/fc48b1388b644c4628e9602ca82139e4?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Messier Tidy Upper
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    @92. andy Says:
                   </p>
                   <blockquote>
                    <p>
                     <i>
                      Incidentally even if Gliese 581g does exist,
                      <b>
                       it is not necessarily habitable.
                      </b>
                      One of the things that you tend to find if you model the interiors of super-Earth planets is that it is very likely that the cores of planets more massive than about 2-3 times the Earths mass are entirely solid. A solid core means no magnetic dynamo. This is not promising for habitability prospects, particularly not around red dwarf stars which experience violent flare activity for a long part of their lifetimes.
                     </i>
                    </p>
                   </blockquote>
                   <p>
                    Very true &amp; good point.
                    <img alt=":-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_smile.gif"/>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Actually with a mass of 3 to 4 x Earths Gliese 581g exceeds the maximum mass limit of 2.35 earth masses suggested by Stephen Dole &amp; Isaac Asimov in their book
                    <i>
                     Planets For Man
                    </i>
                    as quoted in my comment #114 above.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    It seems likely to still be too large, maybe more of a gas dwarf, hot ice or ocean planet. We really still know far too little to domore than just speculate onthe possibility of life there.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    @126.   Chris Winter Says:
                   </p>
                   <blockquote>
                    <p>
                     <i>
                      Phil wrote: What does this mean? Well, thats a whole lot of planets! Thats what it means. I think (read: have faith) that the numbers are going to work out pretty close to your estimate, when we have enough data to make the calculation somewhat solid. A small fraction of those will be earthlike, of course. But if we have the means to get to them in substantial numbers, we will also have the means to terraform a good many of them. Which means that Senate Chamber on Coruscant wont be nearly big enough!
                     </i>
                    </p>
                   </blockquote>
                   <p>
                    Well, not all the habitable planets and sentient species were represented or included in the Old Republic &amp; its Senate  The Hutts and Tattooine for example werent!
                    <img alt=";-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_wink.gif"/>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Also many exoplanets may be suited for life yet not have intelligent species. Frex Earth has only had humanity for a
                    <b>
                     few million years
                    </b>
                    <i>
                     (&amp; only a few centuries technologically advanced)
                    </i>
                    out of its incomprehensibly longer 4.5
                    <b>
                     *billion*
                    </b>
                    year history  and for nearly half our Earths existence bacteria were the only living things here.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    So while there may well be 2.5 billion habitable exoplanets perhaps only a very small fraction of them
                    <i>
                     (10%, 1%, 0.001 %?)
                    </i>
                    will turn out to host intelligent technological life forms on them.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Life, I suspect, may be reasonably common but
                    <b>
                     *sentience*
                    </b>
                    I feel is likely to be extremely exceedingly rare and this may at least in large part be the answer for the Fermi paradox.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    @127.   Chris Winter :
                   </p>
                   <blockquote>
                    <p>
                     <i>
                      The Ward-Brownlee
                     </i>
                     Rare Earth
                     <i>
                      hypothesis is an interesting one. I think its basically right, but at the same time I think the authors are way too pessimistic.
                     </i>
                    </p>
                   </blockquote>
                   <p>
                    I think well probably find the truth lies somewhere beween the extremes of the picture presented by that
                    <i>
                     Rare Earth
                    </i>
                    idea and the life everywhere case argued by  Jack Cohen &amp; Ian Stewart in their
                    <i>
                     Evolving the Alien
                    </i>
                    /
                    <i>
                     What Does a Martian Look Like?
                    </i>
                    book.
                    <img alt=":-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_smile.gif"/>
                   </p>

                    <i>

For a science blog writer to pretend that planetary colonization is a foregone conclusion is irrational and irresponsible. Yes, its logical possible  its also highly improbable.

                   </p>

                    Faith, as you note, is belief without supporting evidence. By definition. Why then do you condemn it for not being evidence-based?
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Also, I have to wonder what sort of evidence for the likelihood of humans making homes on other planets you would accept. Humans have already lived repeatedly (however briefly) on an airless world. They have maintained bases in Antarctica for over half a century. Setting up on Mars (for example) is merely a more challenging problem in logistics.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     That fact that not everyone behaves this way is irrelevant. Collectively we are destroying our future. To say that our problems are not insoluble completely misses point. Were NOT fixing them and the efforts current underway are benign.
                    </i>
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    I would rather state it this way: We are at this period in history allowing certain governments of major nations to follow a path that, if continued, presents a grave risk to the future survival of civilization.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    It does not follow that we, collectively, will continue to follow this destructive path. One bit of evidence: Glenn Becks rally back in August drew an estimated 87,000; the attendance estimate for yesterdays Rally to Restore Sanity is 215,000.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    <i>
                     Youre not responding to my arguments  youre just denying them. The Titanic is headed for an iceberg and youre rearranging deck chairs, pretending that the iceberg really isnt that big. The term cognitive dissonance comes to mind.
                    </i>

Clarke, Olaf Stapledon, Stephen Baxter, David Brin, etc  and also check out the two books mentioned in my comments # 133 &amp; 112 above  arguing for the negative rare ( Ward-Brownlee) &amp; positive everywhere (Cohen-Stewart) cases regarding cosmic life and its abundance.

Human life is always messy and problematic, the poor will always be with us and also wars, etc ..

                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    I think it stands to reason that a civilization which can invent interstellar travel (presumably having cracked the FTL problem) can also colonize/terraform pretty much anything, including large moons such as Titan: one would imagine that initial efforts would be subterranean (subtitanian?) because its cheaper than building a radiation shield around the whole moon (gas giants are typically not healthy things to be close to); this being said, one can also hypothesize that interstellar travel will be seen as a necessity: whereas the get-off-Earth-now crowd are worried about the Earth being hit by, say, a Mars-sized asteroid and generating an extra moon shortly after melting, subsequent generations will be more worried about things like the Sun going nova. In any event, itll be fascinating for the human race a million or so years down the line to look back at these comments (yes, Im fairly sure theyll be archived somewhere) and compare reality to the dreams of the as-yet-uninitiated members of the 21st century, ancient human race.

               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-261454">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261454">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/eca15b2b601e7e577d38bd5210a753ac?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Carl Youngblood
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    &gt;&gt;Youre not responding to my arguments  youre just denying them. The Titanic is headed for an iceberg and youre rearranging deck chairs, pretending that the iceberg really isnt that big. The term cognitive dissonance comes to mind.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    &gt;OK, have it your way. Were all doomed! Doomed, I tells ya!
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Chris brings up a good point. If the only alternative to a far-fetched argument is annihilation then you should choose the far-fetched argument, because it is irrational to choose any path in which destruction is guaranteed. This point was well-articulated by Lincoln Cannon in The New God Argument:
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Consider, for example, the attitude we should take if we were to discover that a large asteroid is headed directly at Earth and will destroy all life on the planet when it hits us in five years. While some of us may languish in despair and others may passively await a chance or supernatural remedy, most of would naturally take up the faith position and begin planning for and building a means of destroying or displacing the asteroid, even while feeling anxious or simultaneously hoping for a lucky break or supernatural assistance.
                    <br/>
                    (
                    <a href="http://lincoln.metacannon.net/2008_08_01_archive.html" rel="nofollow">
                     http://lincoln.metacannon.net/2008_08_01_archive.html
                    </a>
                    )
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    His definition of faith is a little different though. It is not defined as an irrational belief the unprovable. Its more like putting ones trust in posthumanity rather than human extinction. Either alternative is possible, but acceptance of our inevitable doom is irrational, so we should trust in and work towards posthumanity.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261454">
                      November 17, 2010 at 12:19 pm
                     </a>

               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-261462">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261462">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/b2d9c8f540b850761823dd6d4df375e5?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Elvis
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    Getting on one of these planets is not just an engineering problem. Its also a physics problem. If the closest planet thats Earth-like is, lets say 10 light years from here, it would take 10 years to get there on the velocity of light. But then we would have relativity problems, since the time would pass as 10 years for the crew in the ship, but it would be an infinite time for us here, so wed never be able to get feedback from the ship. Besides, you can never reach the speed of light, and nothing guarantees that the human body would survive at such high speed or acceleration to get to a speed so high in a human lifetime, and thats also not an engineering problem. It would also require a lot of energy to get a ship to speeds as high as a decent fraction of the speed of light, more energy than we can get from the resources in the Earth, and thats only part of a big engineering problem. And all that are just a few reasons why its impossible to get there. At the very least, to get to one of these planets, we would have to change many laws of physics, that are supposed to be unbreakable. That doesnt mean someone cant come up with a theory that contradicts Einstein and prove that theory is right, making it basically an engineering problem.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261462">

                     </a>

                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/2227b036075e662a8f2991b0894ee4ac?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Ethan
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    I am with Chris on this one:
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    9.   Chris Says:
                    <br/>
                    October 29th, 2010 at 7:50 am
                    <br/>
                    Isnt there a Goldilocks zone for the galaxy as well? If you get too close to the center there is way too much radiation and too far out less heavy elements to make rocky planets.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Due to the existence of the Galactic Goldilocks Zone, this calculation is therefore a rough calculation.  I recommend limiting the cylindrical volume of the galaxy into a torus that is encompassed by the Galactic Goldilocks Zone.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261463">
                      July 26, 2012 at 11:56 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>
               </li>
               <!-- #comment-## -->
               <li class="comment odd alt thread-odd thread-alt depth-1" id="li-comment-261464">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261464">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://1.gravatar.com/avatar/9802aea33662ed52747747df6db36fd7?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F1.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Saed Nia
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    I agree however if there are identical planets like earth life could be either primitive or way more advanced Cuz there are other factors for evolution and progress
                    <br/>
                    The probability of another earth planet is like finding another human with the exact  DNA.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261464">
                      August 12, 2012 at 12:44 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>
               </li>
               <!-- #comment-## -->
               <li class="comment even thread-even depth-1" id="li-comment-261465">
                <div class="commentwrap clearfix">
                 <div class="commentBox clearfix" id="comment-261465">
                  <div class="comment-author vcard">
                   <img alt="" class="avatar avatar-84 photo" height="84" src="http://0.gravatar.com/avatar/2dc3a60430c289737c24483ee03442b4?s=84&amp;d=http%3A%2F%2F0.gravatar.com%2Favatar%2Fad516503a11cd5ca435acc9bb6523536%3Fs%3D84&amp;r=G" width="84"/>
                   <cite class="fn">
                    Eddie Livingston
                   </cite>
                  </div>
                  <div class="commenttext">
                   <p>
                    It is a vast, vast space and with our puny brains we are not able to comprehend the space we need to cover for travel to even one of these closest planets. Not that it is not possible, but at least not in our lifetime and the lifetimes of a few generations ahead.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Consider these: Energy for travel that long, conditions for human habitat for traveling, human mutation factors, supplies, technology (which we already know is not even passed its infancy), radiation containment and many, many other factors, which we still dont account for.
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    Under all these factors we might as well think about propelling a whole planet (say Earth)  attaching a pretty good engine to it and travel with our resources
                    <img alt=":-)" class="wp-smiley" src="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/wp-includes/images/smilies/icon_smile.gif"/>
                    till we run out (which may happen pretty quickly).
                   </p>
                   <p>
                    So the short answer would be no, we cannot travel that far yet.
                   </p>
                   <div class="comment-meta commentmetadata">
                    <span class="commentdate">
                     <a href="http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/2010/10/29/how-many-habitable-planets-are-there-in-the-galaxy/#comment-261465">
                      September 20, 2012 at 1:31 pm
                     </a>
                    </span>
                    <span class="reply">
                    </span>
                   </div>
                  </div>
                 </div>
                </div>
               </li>
               <!-- #comment-## -->
              </ol>
             </div>

           /* &lt;![CDATA[ */
		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_categories');
			function onCatChange() {
				if ( dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value &gt; 0 ) {
					location.href = "http://blogs.discovermagazine.com/badastronomy/?cat="+dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_tags');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();

		(function() {
			var dropdown = document.getElementById('discoverblog_archives');
			function onCatChange() {
				if (dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value) {
					location.href = dropdown.options[dropdown.selectedIndex].value;
				}
			}
			dropdown.onchange = onCatChange;
		})();
